        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_to_Don_Quixote_de_la_Mancha_by_Miguel_de_Cervantes.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Illustration to Don Quixote de la Mancha by Miguel de Cervantes (the edition translated by Charles Jarvis)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Miguel_de_Cervantes_-_Don_Quixote_-_Part_1_-_Chapter_1_-_Plate_1_%22A_world_of_disorderly_notions,_picked_out_of_his_books,_crowded_into_his_imagination%22.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8352" data-file-width="6456" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Miguel_de_Cervantes_-_Don_Quixote_-_Part_1_-_Chapter_1_-_Plate_1_%22A_world_of_disorderly_notions%2C_picked_out_of_his_books%2C_crowded_into_his_imagination%22.jpg/170px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Miguel_de_Cervantes_-_Don_Quixote_-_Part_1_-_Chapter_1_-_Plate_1_%22A_world_of_disorderly_notions%2C_picked_out_of_his_books%2C_crowded_into_his_imagination%22.jpg/255px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Miguel_de_Cervantes_-_Don_Quixote_-_Part_1_-_Chapter_1_-_Plate_1_%22A_world_of_disorderly_notions%2C_picked_out_of_his_books%2C_crowded_into_his_imagination%22.jpg/340px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gustave_Dor%C3%A9_-_Miguel_de_Cervantes_-_Don_Quixote_-_Part_1_-_Chapter_1_-_Plate_1_%22A_world_of_disorderly_notions,_picked_out_of_his_books,_crowded_into_his_imagination%22.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">
          Gustave Doré
         </a>
         's first (of about 370) illustrations for
         <i>
          Don Quixote
         </i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="928" data-file-width="580" height="272" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg/170px-La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg/255px-La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg/340px-La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:La_Galatea_First_Edition_Title_Page.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Cervantes'
         <i>
          La Galatea
         </i>
         (1585), original title page.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         El ingenioso hidalgo Don Quixote de la Mancha
        </i>
        (1605): First volume of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
          Don Quixote
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Novelas_ejemplares" title="Novelas ejemplares">
          Novelas ejemplares
         </a>
        </i>
        (1613): a collection of 12 short stories of varied types about the social, political, and historical problems of Cervantes' Spain:
        <ul>
         <li>
          "La gitanilla" ("The Gypsy Girl")
         </li>
         <li>
          "El amante liberal" ("The Generous Lover")
         </li>
         <li>
          "Rinconete y Cortadillo" ("Rinconete &amp; Cortadillo")
         </li>
         <li>
          "La española inglesa" ("The English Spanish Lady")
         </li>
         <li>
          "
          <a href="/wiki/El_licenciado_Vidriera" title="El licenciado Vidriera">
           El licenciado Vidriera
          </a>
          " ("The Lawyer of Glass")
         </li>
         <li>
          "La fuerza de la sangre" ("The Power of Blood")
         </li>
         <li>
          "El celoso extremeño" ("The Jealous Man From Extremadura")
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Br_9-14">
           <a href="#cite_note-Br-9">
            [9]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
         <li>
          "La ilustre fregona" ("The Illustrious Kitchen-Maid")
         </li>
         <li>
          "Novela de las dos doncellas" ("The Novel of the Two Damsels")
         </li>
         <li>
          "Novela de la señora Cornelia" ("The Novel of Lady Cornelia")
         </li>
         <li>
          "Novela del casamiento engañoso" ("The Novel of the Deceitful Marriage")
         </li>
         <li>
          "
          <a href="/wiki/El_coloquio_de_los_perros" title="El coloquio de los perros">
           El coloquio de los perros
          </a>
          " ("The Dialogue of the Dogs")
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Segunda Parte del Ingenioso Cavallero [sic] Don Quixote de la Mancha
        </i>
        (1615): Second volume of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
          Don Quixote
         </a>
        </i>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda" title="Los trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda">
          Los trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda
         </a>
        </i>
        (1617).
        <i>
         Persiles
        </i>
        , as it is commonly known, is the best evidence not only of the survival of
        <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_novel" title="Byzantine novel">
         Byzantine novel
        </a>
        themes but also of the survival of forms and ideas of the Spanish novel of the second
        <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
         Renaissance
        </a>
        . In this work, published after the author's death, Cervantes relates the ideal love and unbelievable vicissitudes of a couple, who, starting from the Arctic regions, arrive in Rome, where they find a happy ending to their complicated adventure.
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/La_Galatea" title="La Galatea">
          La Galatea
         </a>
        </i>
        , the
        <a href="/wiki/Pastoral" title="Pastoral">
         pastoral
        </a>
        romance, which Cervantes wrote in his youth, is an imitation of the
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Diana_(pastoral_romance)" title="Diana (pastoral romance)">
          Diana
         </a>
        </i>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Jorge_de_Montemor" title="Jorge de Montemor">
         Jorge de Montemor
        </a>
        and bears an even closer resemblance to
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gil_Polo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gil Polo (page does not exist)">
         Gil Polo
        </a>
        's continuation of that romance. Next to
        <i>
         Don Quixote
        </i>
        and the
        <i>
         Novelas ejemplares
        </i>
        , it is particularly worthy of attention, as it manifests the poetic direction in which Cervantes moved at an early period of life.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Don_Quixote">
        <i>
         Don Quixote
        </i>
        <span id="Don_Quixote">
        </span>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Don Quixote">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
        Don Quixote
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Don_Quixote.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="736" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Don_Quixote.jpg/170px-Don_Quixote.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Don_Quixote.jpg/255px-Don_Quixote.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Don_Quixote.jpg/340px-Don_Quixote.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Don_Quixote.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Statuettes of Don Quixote (left) and Sancho Panza (right).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       (spelled "Quijote" in modern Spanish) is two separate volumes, now nearly always published as one, that cover the adventures of Don Quixote de la Mancha, a hero who carries his enthusiasm and self-deception to unintentional and comic ends. On one level,
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       works as a
       <a href="/wiki/Satire" title="Satire">
        satire
       </a>
       of the romances of
       <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">
        chivalry
       </a>
       , which, though still popular in Cervantes' time, had become an object of ridicule among more demanding critics. The choice of a madman as hero also served a critical purpose, for it was "the impression of ill-being or 'in-sanity,' rather than a finding of dementia or psychosis in clinical terms, that defined the madman for Cervantes and his contemporaries." Indeed, the concept of madness was "associated with physical or moral displacement, as may be seen in the literal and figurative sense of the adjectives
       <i>
        eccentric
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        extravagant
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        deviant
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        aberrant
       </i>
       , etc."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The novel allows Cervantes to illuminate various aspects of human nature. Because the novel, particularly the first part, was written in individually published sections, the composition includes several incongruities. Cervantes pointed out some of these errors in the preface to the second part; but he disdained to correct them, because he conceived that they had been too severely condemned by his critics. Cervantes felt a passion for the vivid painting of character.
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       is noble-minded, an enthusiastic admirer of everything good and great, yet having all these fine qualities accidentally blended with a relative kind of madness. He is paired with a character of opposite qualities,
       <a href="/wiki/Sancho_Panza" title="Sancho Panza">
        Sancho Panza
       </a>
       , a man of low self-esteem, who is a compound of grossness and simplicity.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       is cited as the first classic model of the modern romance or novel, and it has served as the prototype of the comic novel. The humorous situations are mostly burlesque, and it includes satire.
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       is one of the
       <i>
        Encyclopædia Britannica'
       </i>
       s
       <a href="/wiki/Great_Books_of_the_Western_World" title="Great Books of the Western World">
        Great Books of the Western World
       </a>
       , while the Russian author
       <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky">
        Fyodor Dostoyevsky
       </a>
       called it "the ultimate and most sublime work of human thinking".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is in
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       that Cervantes coined the popular phrase "the proof of the pudding is in the eating" (
       <i>
        por la muestra se conoce el paño
       </i>
       ), which still sees heavy use in the shortened form of "the proof is in the pudding", and "who walks much and reads much, knows much and sees much" (
       <i>
        quien anda mucho y lee mucho, sabe mucho y ve mucho
       </i>
       ).
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Novelas_ejemplares_.28Exemplary_Novels.29">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Novelas_ejemplares_(Exemplary_Novels)">
        <i>
         Novelas ejemplares
        </i>
        (
        <i>
         Exemplary Novels
        </i>
        )
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Novelas ejemplares (Exemplary Novels)">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Novelas_ejemplares" title="Novelas ejemplares">
        Novelas ejemplares
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1457" data-file-width="1137" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg/170px-Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg/255px-Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg/340px-Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Don_Quijote_illustrated_by_Gustav_Dore_II.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Don Quijote illustrated by Paul Gustave Louis Christophe Doré
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1445" data-file-width="1125" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg/170px-Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg/255px-Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg/340px-Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Paul_Gustave_Louis_Christophe_Dor%C3%A9_III.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Don Quijote (Don Quixote) illustration by Paul Gustave Louis Christophe Doré.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Cervantes intended his novels should be to Spanish nearly what the
       <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">
        novellas
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Boccaccio" title="Giovanni Boccaccio">
        Boccaccio
       </a>
       were to Italians.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Novelas_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Novelas-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Some are anecdotes, some are romances in miniature, some are serious, some comic; they are written in a light, smooth, conversational style.
      </p>
      <p>
       Four novelas, though favourites in Cervantes' day, are perhaps of less interest today than the rest:
       <i>
        El amante liberal
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        La señora Cornelia
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Las dos doncellas
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        La española inglesa
       </i>
       . The common theme to these is pairs of lovers (couples) separated by lamentable and complicated events; they are finally reunited and find the happiness they have longed for. The heroines are all beautiful and of perfect behavior; they and their lovers are capable of the highest sacrifices; and they try to elevate themselves to the ideal of moral and aristocratic distinction which illuminates their lives. In
       <i>
        El amante liberal
       </i>
       , the beautiful Leonisa and her lover Ricardo are carried off by Turkish pirates. Both fight against serious material and moral dangers. Ricardo conquers all obstacles, returns to his homeland with Leonisa, and is ready to renounce his passion and to hand her over to her former lover in an outburst of generosity; but Leonisa's preference naturally settles on Ricardo in the end.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another group of "exemplary" novels is formed by
       <i>
        La fuerza de la sangre
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        La ilustre fregona
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        La gitanilla
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        El celoso extremeño
       </i>
       . The first three offer examples of love and adventure happily resolved, though only on the surface and in purely social terms, for the marriages that occur at their conclusions are morally deficient and repugnant to discerning readers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The last of the four unravels itself tragically. It deals with the aged Felipe Carrizales, who, after travelling widely and becoming rich in America, decides to marry, taking all the precautions necessary to forestall being betrayed, as he had in his youth. He weds a very young girl and isolates her from the world by enclosing her in a well-guarded house with no windows. In spite of these defensive measures, a bold youth succeeds in penetrating the fortress of conjugal honour and one day Carrizales surprises his wife asleep in the arms of this would-be seducer. Carrizales mistakenly assumes that his wife has succumbed to the youth's advances, whereas in fact she did not. Nevertheless, Carrizales pardons those whom he believes to be adulterers, recognizing that he is more to blame than they, and dies of sorrow over the grievous error he has committed. Cervantes here calls into question the social ideal of
       <a href="/wiki/Honour" title="Honour">
        honour
       </a>
       to privilege instead free will and the value of the individual.
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Novelas_ejemplares" title="Novelas ejemplares">
         Rinconete y Cortadillo
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        El casamiento engañoso
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        El licenciado Vidriera
       </i>
       , and the untitled novella known today as
       <i>
        El coloquio de los perros
       </i>
       , four works of art which are concerned more with the personalities of the characters than with the subject matter, form the final group of stories. The protagonists are, respectively, two young vagabonds, Pedro del Rincón and Diego Cortado, Lieutenant Campuzano, a student – Tomás Rodaja (who goes mad and believes he has become glass, and who makes many remarks on society and customs of the time) and finally two dogs, Cipión and Berganza, whose wandering existence serves as a commentary on the picaresque novel.
      </p>
      <p>
       The two young vagabonds of
       <i>
        Rinconete y Cortadillo
       </i>
       come by chance to Seville, and also attracted by the riches and disorder that 16th-century commerce with the Americas had brought to that metropolis. There they come into contact with a brotherhood of thieves, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thieves%27_Guild" title="Thieves' Guild">
        Thieves' Guild
       </a>
       , led by Monipodio, whose house is the headquarters of the Sevillian underworld. The solemn ritual of this band of ruffians is all the more comic for being presented in Cervantes' drily humorous style.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Los_Trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda">
        <i>
         Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda" title="Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda">
        Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_(1617).png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="586" data-file-width="350" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png/170px-Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png/255px-Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png/340px-Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_%281617%29.png 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Los_trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda_(1617).png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Title page of Persiles and Segismunda.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Cervantes finished the romance of
       <i>
        The Labours of Persiles and Sigismunda
       </i>
       shortly before his death. The idea of this romance was not new and Cervantes appears to imitate
       <a href="/wiki/Heliodorus_of_Emesa" title="Heliodorus of Emesa">
        Heliodorus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The work is a romantic description of travels, both by sea and land. Real and fabulous geography and history are mixed together and, in the second half of the romance, the scene is transferred to Spain and Italy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Poetry">
        Poetry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Poetry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Some of his poems are found in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/La_Galatea" title="La Galatea">
         La Galatea
        </a>
        .
       </i>
       He also wrote
       <i>
        Dos Canciones à la Armada Invencible
       </i>
       . His best work, however, is found in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sonnets" title="Sonnets">
        sonnets
       </a>
       , particularly
       <i>
        Al Túmulo del Rey Felipe en Sevilla
       </i>
       . Among his most important poems,
       <i>
        Canto de Calíope
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        Epístola a Mateo Vázquez
       </i>
       , and the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Viaje_del_Parnaso" title="Viaje del Parnaso">
         Viaje del Parnaso
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Journey to Parnassus
       </i>
       – 1614) stand out. The latter is his most ambitious work in verse, an
       <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">
        allegory
       </a>
       which consists largely of reviews of contemporary poets. Compared to his ability as a novelist, Cervantes is often considered a mediocre poet.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Viaje_del_Parnaso">
        <i>
         Viaje del Parnaso
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Viaje del Parnaso">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="736" data-file-width="463" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg/170px-Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg/255px-Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg/340px-Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viaje_del_Parnaso.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Frontispiece of the
         <i>
          Viaje
         </i>
         (1614).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The prose of the
       <i>
        Galatea
       </i>
       , which is in other respects so beautiful, is occasionally overloaded with epithet. Cervantes displays a totally different kind of poetic talent in the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Viaje_del_Parnaso" title="Viaje del Parnaso">
         Viaje del Parnaso
        </a>
       </i>
       , an extended commentary on the Spanish authors of his time.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plays">
        Plays
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Plays">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Comparisons to more famous playwrights, those that use more popular structures and have more authority over the world of theatre, have diminished the reputation of his plays; but two of them (
       <i>
        El Trato de Argel
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Numancia" title="La Numancia">
         La Numancia
        </a>
       </i>
       – 1582) made an impact.
       <i>
        El Trato de Argel
       </i>
       is written in 5 acts; based on his experiences as a captive of the Moors, the play deals with the life of Christian slaves in Algiers.
       <i>
        La Numancia
       </i>
       is a description of the siege of Numantia by the Romans. It details the horrors of the siege, and has been described as devoid of the requisites of dramatic art (he ignores structure and changes scheme and syllables per line many times throughout). Cervantes's output published in his lifetime consists of 16 dramatic works including eight full-length plays (Spanish links to plays included):
      </p>
      <div style="clear:left;">
      </div>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         El gallardo español
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Los baños de Argel
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         La Gran Sultana, Doña Catalina de Oviedo
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         La casa de los celos
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         El laberinto de amor
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
         <a href="#cite_note-49">
          [49]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         La entretenida
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
         <a href="#cite_note-50">
          [50]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         El rufián dichoso
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
         <a href="#cite_note-51">
          [51]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Pedro de Urdemalas
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
         <a href="#cite_note-52">
          [52]
         </a>
        </sup>
        a sensitive play about a
        <i>
         picaro
        </i>
        , who joins a group of Gypsies for love of a girl.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       He also wrote 8 short farces (
       <a href="/wiki/Entrem%C3%A9s" title="Entremés">
        <i>
         entremeses
        </i>
       </a>
       ):
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <i>
         El juez de los divorcios
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
         <a href="#cite_note-53">
          [53]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         El rufián viudo llamado Trampagos
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
         <a href="#cite_note-54">
          [54]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         La elección de los Alcaldes de Daganzo
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
         <a href="#cite_note-55">
          [55]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         La guarda cuidadosa
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioteca.org.ar_56-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-biblioteca.org.ar-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (The Vigilant Sentinel),
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-biblioteca.org.ar_56-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-biblioteca.org.ar-56">
          [56]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         El vizcaíno fingido
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
         <a href="#cite_note-57">
          [57]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         El retablo de las maravillas
        </i>
        ,
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
         <a href="#cite_note-58">
          [58]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/La_cueva_de_Salamanca_(The_Cave_of_Salamanca)" title="La cueva de Salamanca (The Cave of Salamanca)">
          La cueva de Salamanca
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
       </li>
       <li>
        <i>
         Del viejo celoso
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
         <a href="#cite_note-59">
          [59]
         </a>
        </sup>
        (The Jealous Old Man).
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       These plays and entremeses made up
       <i>
        Ocho Comedias y ocho entire messes nuevos, nunca representados
       </i>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (
       <i>
        Eight Comedies and Eight New Interludes, Never Before Performed
       </i>
       ) which appeared in 1615. The dates and order of composition of Cervantes' entremeses are unknown. Faithful to the spirit of Lope de Rueda, Cervantes endowed them with novelistic elements, such as simplified plot, the type of descriptions normally associated with a novel, and character development. Cervantes included some of his dramas among the works he was most satisfied with.
      </p>
      <p>
       It is believed that he above all wanted to be a dramatist, to see his plays reach fruition. He thought that if only people with standing would view his works, they would see what he had to offer; the evidence is so strong for this that, "In all probability he would have given all the success of 'Don Quixote,' nay, would have seen every copy of 'Don Quixote' burned in the Plaza Mayor, for one such success as
       <a href="/wiki/Lope_de_Vega" title="Lope de Vega">
        Lope de Vega
       </a>
       was enjoying on an average once a week."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proyecto_Cervantes_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Proyecto_Cervantes-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       His lack of care for Don Quixote when it was published further illustrates this desire for theatrical glory. The novel, initially uncelebrated by its author, was strung together and offered to its audience as a literary trifle whose object was merely to relieve boredom or act as a springboard towards other ideas, despite the profound legacy it would later develop.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Proyecto_Cervantes_61-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Proyecto_Cervantes-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="La_Numancia">
        <i>
         La Numancia
        </i>
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: La Numancia">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/The_Siege_of_Numantia" title="The Siege of Numantia">
        The Siege of Numantia
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       This play is a dramatization of the long and brutal siege of the
       <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">
        Celtiberian
       </a>
       town Numantia,
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">
        Hispania
       </a>
       , by the Roman forces of
       <a href="/wiki/Scipio_Africanus" title="Scipio Africanus">
        Scipio Africanus
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Cervantes invented, along with the subject of his piece, a peculiar style of tragic composition; and, in doing so, he did not pay much regard to the theory of Aristotle. His object was to produce a piece full of tragic situations, combined with the charm of the marvellous. In order to accomplish this goal, Cervantes relied heavily on allegory and on mythological elements.
      </p>
      <p>
       The tragedy is written in conformity with no rules, save those which the author prescribed for himself, for he felt no inclination to imitate the Greek forms. The play is divided into four acts,
       <i>
        jornadas
       </i>
       ; and no chorus is introduced. The dialogue is sometimes in tercets, and sometimes in
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Redondilla&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Redondilla (page does not exist)">
        <i>
         redondillas
        </i>
       </a>
       , and for the most part in octaves – without any regard to rule.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
        Legacy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Legacy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="782" data-file-width="492" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg/170px-Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg/255px-Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg/340px-Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_de_Cervantes_Saavedra_01.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Miguel de Cervantes in a late and idealized portrait of the 18th century (
         <i>
          Retratos de Españoles Ilustres
         </i>
         -
         <i>
          Portraits of Illustrious Spanish
         </i>
         , 1791).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1710" data-file-width="1368" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg/170px-Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg/255px-Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg/340px-Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Miguel_cervantes_de_saavedra.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Cervantes: Image from a 19th-century German book on the history of literature.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       During his life, Cervantes was primarily known as a writer of comedy, which was how
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
         Don Quixote
        </a>
       </i>
       Part I was viewed in his day.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       His
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exemplary_Novels" title="Exemplary Novels">
        Exemplary Novels
       </a>
       were better received
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       than
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
         Don Quixote
        </a>
       </i>
       and allowed him to publish his plays and
       <i>
        entremeses
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Viaje_del_Parnaso" title="Viaje del Parnaso">
         Viaje del Parnaso
        </a>
       </i>
       , Part II of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
         Don Quixote
        </a>
       </i>
       , and (by his widow)
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Los_Trabajos_de_Persiles_y_Sigismunda" title="Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda">
        Los Trabajos de Persiles y Sigismunda
       </a>
       . He then faded into semi-obscurity.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       Interest in him first revived in England in the 18th century, first by the deluxe edition of Tonson, for which the first biographical sketch was written (1738), and then by the scholarly editor
       <a href="/wiki/John_Bowle_(writer)" title="John Bowle (writer)">
        John Bowle (writer)
       </a>
       , who was the first to call Cervantes a "classic" author.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       This began his influence on other novelists and then an intense interest by the German
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romantics" title="Romantics">
        romantics
       </a>
       in the late 18th and early 19th centuries.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       In 1805 (the bicentennial of the publication of
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote" title="Don Quixote">
         Don Quixote
        </a>
       </i>
       Part I), and 1816 (the bicentennial of Cervantes' death), nothing happened. The tricentenial, 1905, saw a great wave of celebrations in Spain.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The year 2016, the 400th anniversary of Cervantes' death, saw the production of
       <i>
        Cervantina
       </i>
       , a celebration of the author's plays by the Compañía Nacional de Teatro Clásico in Madrid.
      </p>
      <p>
       Cervantes' novel
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       has had a tremendous influence on the development of prose fiction. It has been translated into all major languages and has appeared in 700 editions. The first translation was in English, made by
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Shelton_(translator)" title="Thomas Shelton (translator)">
        Thomas Shelton
       </a>
       in 1608 (Part I only
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       ), but not published until 1612. Shelton renders some Spanish idioms into English so literally that they sound nonsensical when translated.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       As an example Shelton always translates the word
       <i>
        dedos
       </i>
       as
       <i>
        fingers
       </i>
       , not realizing that
       <i>
        dedos
       </i>
       can also mean
       <i>
        inches
       </i>
       . (In the original Spanish, for instance, a phrase such as
       <i>
        una altura de quince dedos
       </i>
       , which makes perfect sense in Spanish, would mean "fifteen inches high" in English, but a translator who renders it too literally would translate it as "fifteen
       <i>
        fingers
       </i>
       high".
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Fuentes" title="Carlos Fuentes">
        Carlos Fuentes
       </a>
       raised the possibility that Cervantes and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shakespeare" title="Shakespeare">
        Shakespeare
       </a>
       were the same person, in the sense that
       <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">
        Homer
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">
        Dante
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">
        Defoe
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dickens" title="Dickens">
        Dickens
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balzac" title="Balzac">
        Balzac
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">
        Joyce
       </a>
       are all the same writer whose spirit wanders through the centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Fuentes noted that "Cervantes leaves the page open where the reader knows himself read and the author written."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">
        Sigmund Freud
       </a>
       was greatly influenced by "
       <i>
        El coloquio de los perros
       </i>
       ", which has been called the origin of psychoanalysis. In it, only one character tells his story; the other listens, occasionally making comments. At the center of the dog's story is a sexual event. Freud stated that he learned Spanish so as to read Cervantes in the original, and he signed 55 letters with the name of the character (dog) Cipión.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Virtual_Miguel_de_Cervantes" title="Biblioteca Virtual Miguel de Cervantes">
        Biblioteca Virtual Miguel de Cervantes
       </a>
       , the largest
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Digital_archive" title="Digital archive">
        digital archive
       </a>
       of Spanish-language historical and literary works in the world, is named after the author.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="References_in_other_works">
        References in other works
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: References in other works">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Don Quixote
       </i>
       has been the subject of a variety of works in other fields of art, including:
      </p>
      <h4>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">
        Literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <i>
        Don Quixote'
       </i>
       s influence can be seen in the work of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Defoe" title="Daniel Defoe">
         Daniel Defoe
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Henry_Fielding" title="Henry Fielding">
         Henry Fielding
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Tobias_Smollett" title="Tobias Smollett">
         Tobias Smollett
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Laurence_Sterne" title="Laurence Sterne">
         Laurence Sterne
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The classic 19th-century novelists
        <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">
         Charles Dickens
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fyodor_Dostoevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoevsky">
         Fyodor Dostoevsky
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">
         Gustave Flaubert
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Herman_Melville" title="Herman Melville">
         Herman Melville
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Walter_Scott" title="Sir Walter Scott">
         Sir Walter Scott
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Mark_Twain" title="Mark Twain">
         Mark Twain
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        The 20th-century works of
        <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Luis_Borges" title="Jorge Luis Borges">
         Jorge Luis Borges
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Giannina_Braschi" title="Giannina Braschi">
         Giannina Braschi
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/James_Joyce" title="James Joyce">
         James Joyce
        </a>
        .
       </li>
      </ul>

      <h5>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Opera">
        Opera
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Opera">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h5>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Georg_Philipp_Telemann" title="Georg Philipp Telemann">
         Georg Philipp Telemann
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Don_Quichotte_auf_der_Hochzeit_des_Comacho" title="Don Quichotte auf der Hochzeit des Comacho">
          Don Quichotte auf der Hochzeit des Comacho
         </a>
        </i>
        (1761)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Paisiello" title="Giovanni Paisiello">
         Giovanni Paisiello
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         Don Chisciotte della Mancia
        </i>
        (1769)
       </li>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Salieri" title="Antonio Salieri">
         Antonio Salieri
        </a>
        :
        <i>
         Don Chisciotte alle nozze di Gamace
        </i>
        (1770/1771)
       </li>
       <li>

      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_art">
        Visual art
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Visual art">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <ul>
       <li>
        <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">
         Pablo Picasso
        </a>
        painted a
        <a href="/wiki/Don_Quixote_(Picasso)" title="Don Quixote (Picasso)">
         picture
        </a>
        illustrating a scene from
        <i>
         Don Quixote.
        </i>
       </li>
       <li>
        In 1965,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvador_Dali" title="Salvador Dali">
         Salvador Dali
        </a>
        created a Cervantes etching as part of the "Five Spanish Immortals' series (also included El Cid, El Greco, Velazquez, and Don Quixote).
       </li>
       <li>
        The theme of the novel also inspired the 19th-century French artists
        <a href="/wiki/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier" title="Honoré Daumier">
         Honoré Daumier
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Doré">
         Gustave Doré
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         [
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
           <span title="entire paragraph (March 2015)">
            citation needed
           </span>
          </a>
         </i>
         ]
        </sup>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Place">
        Place
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Place">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Cervantes,_Ilocos_Sur" title="Cervantes, Ilocos Sur">
        Cervantes
       </a>
       was named after him.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethnic_and_religious_heritage">
        Ethnic and religious heritage
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ethnic and religious heritage">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Modern scholars have suggested that he may have descended from a
       <a href="/wiki/New_Christian" title="New Christian">
        New Christian
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Converso" title="Converso">
        Converso
       </a>
       background, i.e., that his ancestors, prior to 1492, had been Jews.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Leandro Rodriguez of the
       <a href="/wiki/University_of_Lausanne" title="University of Lausanne">
        University of Lausanne
       </a>
       (
       <i>
        Don Miguel, Judío de Cervantes
       </i>
       , 1992) has written that the places, foliage, distances and sounds in the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Cervantes,_Lugo" title="Cervantes, Lugo">
        Cervantes, Lugo
       </a>
       prove that the setting for the Don Quixote story took place there. Rodriguez also says that "Saavedra" is believed to refer to the area of Spain known as La Mancha, but was rather set near
       <a href="/wiki/Zamora,_Spain" title="Zamora, Spain">
        Zamora, Spain
       </a>
       and that la mancha ("the stain") refers to his converso ("forced convert from Judaism") background.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Advocates of the New Christian theory, first set forth by
       <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%A9rico_Castro" title="Américo Castro">
        Américo Castro
       </a>
       , often suggest Cervantes' mother was a converso as well. The theory rests almost exclusively on circumstantial evidence but would explain some mysteries of Cervantes' life.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been supported by authors such as Anthony Cascardi
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and Eisenberg.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Others, such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Claudio_S%C3%A1nchez-Albornoz_y_Mendui%C3%B1a" title="Claudio Sánchez-Albornoz y Menduiña">
        Claudio Sánchez-Albornoz
       </a>
       (or Francisco Olmos Garcia, who considers it a "tired issue" and only supported by Américo Castro) reject the theory strongly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One forgotten fact remains that the monastery of Saint Daniel in Hadath El Joubbeh in northern Lebanon harbors a rare document of a noblemen family of Karam tracing back Cervantes to an ancient patriarch, Simonus De Karam Cervantos Kfar Chmim, locally thought to be the great-grandfather of the author and a pillar of early Spanish linguistics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Likeness">
        Likeness
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Likeness">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cervantes_Burgos.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3968" data-file-width="2976" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Cervantes_Burgos.JPG/170px-Cervantes_Burgos.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Cervantes_Burgos.JPG/255px-Cervantes_Burgos.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Cervantes_Burgos.JPG/340px-Cervantes_Burgos.JPG 2x" width="170"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cervantes_Burgos.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Bust of Cervantes erected in 1905,
         <a href="/wiki/Burgos" title="Burgos">
          Burgos
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Although the portrait of Cervantes attributed to
       <a href="/wiki/Juan_de_J%C3%A1uregui" title="Juan de Jáuregui">
        Juan de Jáuregui
       </a>
       is the one most associated with the author, there is no known portrait that is really a true likeness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The oil painting
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Retrato_de_un_caballero_desconocido" title="Retrato de un caballero desconocido">
         Retrato de un caballero desconocido
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        Portrait of an Unknown Gentleman
       </i>
       ), painted by
       <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">
        El Greco
       </a>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Toledo,_Spain" title="Toledo, Spain">
        Toledo
       </a>
       between 1600 and 1605, and on display at the
       <a href="/wiki/Museo_del_Prado" title="Museo del Prado">
        Museo del Prado
       </a>
       , has also been cited as a possible portrait of Cervantes, based on the fact that he was living near Toledo in 1604 and that he knew people within El Greco's circle of friends.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1859 portrait by
       <a href="/wiki/Luis_de_Madrazo" title="Luis de Madrazo">
        Luis de Madrazo
       </a>
       , which the painter himself stated is based on his own imagination, is currently at the
       <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Nacional_de_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Biblioteca Nacional de España">
        Biblioteca Nacional de España
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_euro_coins" title="Spanish euro coins">
        Spanish euro coins
       </a>
       of €0.10, €0.20, and €0.50 bear an image of a bust of Cervantes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
        Notes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Miguel_de_Cervantes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">
       <p>
        <b>
         a.
        </b>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Anone">
         <a href="#ref_Anone">
          <b>
           <sup>
            ^
           </sup>
          </b>
         </a>
        </span>
        The most reliable and accurate portrait of the writer is that provided by Cervantes himself in the prologue of the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exemplary_Novels" title="Exemplary Novels">
         Exemplary Novels
        </a>
        , complaining that the lost portrait by
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juan_Mart%C3%ADnez_de_J%C3%A1uregui_y_Aguilar" title="Juan Martínez de Jáuregui y Aguilar">
         Juan Martínez de Jáuregui y Aguilar
        </a>
        was not used as a frontispiece. (translated by Walter K. Kelly):
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gut_81-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Gut-81">
          [81]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <blockquote class="templatequote">
        <p>
         This person whom you see here, with an oval visage, chestnut hair, smooth open forehead, lively eyes, a hooked but well-proportioned nose, and silvery beard that twenty years ago was golden, large moustaches, a small mouth, teeth not much to speak of, for he has but six, in bad condition and worse placed, no two of them corresponding to each other, a figure midway between the two extremes, neither tall nor short, a vivid complexion, rather fair than dark, somewhat stooped in the shoulders, and not very lightfooted: this, I say, is the author of
         <i>
          Galatea
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          Don Quixote de la Mancha
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          The Journey to Parnassus
         </i>
         , which he wrote in imitation of Cesare Caporali Perusino, and other works which are current among the public, and perhaps without the author's name. He is commonly called MIGUEL DE CERVANTES SAAVEDRA.
        </p>
        <div class="templatequotecite">
         —
         <cite>
          Miguel de Cervantes
         </cite>
        </div>
       </blockquote>
       <p>
        <b>
         b.
        </b>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Bnone">
         <a href="#ref_Bnone">
          <b>
           <sup>
            ^
           </sup>
          </b>
         </a>
        </span>
        His signature spells
        <i>
         Cerbantes
        </i>
        with a
        <i>
         b
        </i>
        ; but he is now known after the spelling
        <i>
         Cervantes
        </i>
        , used by the printers of his works. The patronymic of his mother was
        <i>
         Cortinas
        </i>
        .
        <i>
         Saavedra
        </i>
        was the surname of a distant relative. He adopted it as his second surname after his return from the
        <a href="/wiki/Barbary_Coast" title="Barbary Coast">
         Barbary Coast
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garcés_22-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garcés-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         :
         <span>
          191–192
         </span>
        </sup>
        * C. Slade,
        <i>
         Introduction
        </i>
        , xxiv The earliest documents signed with Cervantes' two names,
        <i>
         Cervantes Saavedra
        </i>
        , appear several years after his repatriation. He began adding the second surname (
        <i>
         Saavedra
        </i>
        , a name that did not correspond to his immediate family) to his patronymic in 1586–1587 in official documents related to his marriage to Catalina de Salazar.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Garcés_22-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-Garcés-22">
          [22]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
         :
         <span>
          191–192
         </span>
        </sup>
       </p>
       <p>
        <b>
         c.
        </b>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Cnone">
         <a href="#ref_Cnone">
          <b>
           <sup>
            ^
           </sup>
          </b>
         </a>
        </span>
        The only evidence is a statement by Professor
        <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tomas_Gonz%C3%A1lez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tomas González (page does not exist)">
         Tomas González
        </a>
        , that he once saw an old entry of the matriculation of a Miguel de Cervantes.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrFiK_21-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-OrFiK-21">
          [21]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
         <a href="#cite_note-82">
          [82]
         </a>
        </sup>
        No subsequent scholar has been successful in verifying this statement. In any case, there were at least two other Miguels born about the middle of the century.
       </p>
       <p>
        <b>
         d.
        </b>
        <span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Dnone">
         <a href="#ref_Dnone">
          <b>
           <sup>
            ^
           </sup>
          </b>
         </a>
        </span>
        "He" refers to the writer of a spurious Part II of Don Quixote (
        <i>
         Second Volume of the Ingenious Gentleman Don Quixote of La Mancha
        </i>
        ) known under the
        <a href="/wiki/Pseudonym" title="Pseudonym">
         pseudonym
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Alonso_Fern%C3%A1ndez_de_Avellaneda" title="Alonso Fernández de Avellaneda">
         Alonso Fernández de Avellaneda
        </a>
        . Avellaneda had referred to Cervantes as an "old and one-handed" man.
       </p>
      </div>

        <li id="cite_note-Novelas-42">
         <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
          <b>
           <a href="#cite_ref-Novelas_42-0">
            ^
           </a>
          </b>
         </span>
         <span class="reference-text">
          . The Spanish title of
          <i>
           novelas
          </i>
          is misleading. In modern Spanish it means
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novels" title="Novels">
           novels
          </a>
          , but Cervantes used it to mean the shorter Italian
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Novella" title="Novella">
            novella
           </a>
          </i>
          . See the
          <a href="/wiki/Novel" title="Novel">
           Novel
          </a>
          article for the terminological problem.
         </span>
        </li>

      <div class="refbegin columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">

         , ed. Roberto González Echevarría, 2005

         ] 124, 2 (2009): 372-403.

          ISSN

         </a>
         , Nº 328, 1977, pp. 101–115.

        <li>

         <a class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/32654689/What_is_Don_Quijote_Don_Quixote_And...And...And_The_Disjunctive_Synthesis_of_Cervantes_and_Kathy_Acker" rel="nofollow">
